1 play / 4 players

LIKE: Amazing, colorful artwork. The game mechanisms were interesting, and the variety of cards were interesting. Multiple strategies were apparent. Rounds went quick.
DISLIKE: Light. The storytelling could possibly get repetitive, but that would take quite a few plays.

OVERALL: There is enough interesting things going on in the game that keep your interest. The storytelling aspect is an interesting interruption, one that could get boring after several plays. Yet it is an interruption, one that slows down the pace. Overall, it's a laid-back game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

I had heard good things about this game going into my first play, so my expectations were high.  Unfortunately, I came away a bit disappointed.  The game was enjoyable and seems well designed, but didn't live up to expectations.

The central mechanism of the game is exploring below ground with its book of adventures, and I admit I was expecting more of this element going in.  The adventures are well-written little tales of whimsical adventures that seem to be very family-friendly, but I had been expecting a bit more differentiation in how the adventures are resolved.  As it is, nearly all of the adventures play out the same way: you get a choice of what to do, each with its own number of successes needed in order to achieve the desired result.  You then roll dice for your characters that you sent on the adventure to try to achieve the number of successes.  There are some means of mitigating the randomness of the dice, but really that's all there is to it.

My disappointment with this implementation is that all of the adventures feel samey.  You don't have specialized skills on your characters that push you to choose one path over another, and there really isn't any doubt as to which path is better.  The path with the higher success threshold is (as far as I encountered) always better than the path with the lower threshold.  There are no moral decisions to be made, no roleplaying elements to the decisions.  Your only real decision point is deciding how much you want to push your luck to try to achieve the better outcome.  You could just as easily skip reading the story text and simply list the difficulty numbers, and there would be little effect on the gameplay itself.

I like the conceit of the game, but I would like to see it taken a step further.  The designer has just scratched the surface of what is possible with this kind of game.  I hope that he will put more meat on the bones in a future design that uses this idea of a story-based adventure game.

On a completely separate note, I have to take issue with one small design decision in this game.  When a player goes exploring, the player whose turn is next is the one who reads the story snippet.  This seems a poor decision to me.  I think it should be the player whose turn it was previously should be the one to read the snippet so that the next player can continue thinking about what they will do on their turn as soon as the current player's turn is finished.  This would help the game move faster and reduce down time.

RATING: 5
My family aren't really gamers. I thought the story element would draw them in but it didn't. (We only played once)
Here's what I think about the game. While it seems that many enjoy the adventuring part of the game, it doesn't seem essential to game play. Also the choices are a bit confusing as they're written. Most have 2 choices with 1 or 2 target values each you can shoot for. This is expressed verbally. Not the hardest thing to track but it would be easier if the target values weren't given or were read first. I also think the values take away from the game thematically.
There's no continuous narrative since you send groups of people exploring and they return at the end of the turn. They don't acquire experience, items or improve stats. (You do find items, but you don't equip your party) There's not a risk/reward system where say exploring deeper or longer results in better rewards. Instead you can send more people and/or hire better people to increase the odds of pulling off harder objectives (higher target number). Tales of the Arabian Nights doesn't have much of a game to it but it does have the narrative of a series of adventures that affect the character and collectively tells a story.

RATING: 9.5
Full disclosure: with Ryan and Malorie Laukat, I am one of the storybook writers for Above and Below's encounter book. Fun fun! (I am much enjoying my explorations in the clever and exciting encounters created by both Ryan and Malorie...)

Ryan continues to impress me to no end. Above and Below makes for a refreshing hybrid of quasi-worker placement and storytelling adventure, overflowing with characteristic Ryan Laukat charm. Thick tiles and colorful, quirky artwork (especially that Totoro-esque Cave Cat!) make you feel part of a fantastic over- and under- world. Rules are clean and intuitive, and components high quality and lavishly illustrated. 

My only gripe with the game is that in my plays so far it seems to end too quickly -- at least with two players. We have a suggested "Below and Above" game-lengthening variant here: https://boardgamegeek.com/article/20872823#20872823

For the Exploration outcomes, I think Ryan was wise to (in many cases) leave off explanations for the rewards given to the player. This gives the reader a chance to improvise clever or funny responses of her own as emergent storytelling. (Maybe an outstanding bit of role-playing warrants a coin or a bonus point?)

I look forward to many, many more games. Thank you, Ryan, for such a delight -- and for putting me in the game as one of the villagers!

RATING: N/A
Above and Below is a mashup of town-building and storytelling where you and up to three friends compete to build the best village above and below ground. In the game, you send your villagers to perform jobs like exploring the cave, harvesting resources, and constructing houses. Each villager has unique skills and abilities, and you must decide how to best use them. You have your own personal village board, and you slide the villagers on this board to various areas to indicate that they've been given jobs to do. Will you send Hanna along on the expedition to the cave? Or should she instead spend her time teaching important skills to one of the young villagers?

RATING: 3
Ryan Laukat is a wonderfully talented individual, but in my humble opinion his talents do not include game design. He is an incredible artist and a solid graphic designer, but his games leave me with a sour taste in my mouth at best. This in particular is such an interesting setup, and the resource collection that leads to scaling VP and money generation is great, but the implementation of the overall game is just so incredibly frustrating that it is mind boggling. 

Here is how it works:Go exploring (using at least two of your 3-4 workers to do so). Read a random passage that has NOTHING to do with anything whatsoever. Nothing to do with the people you sent on the mission, nothing to do with anything you have done so far in the game, nothing to do with the buildings you've built, nothing to do with ANYTHING, it is simply a passage chosen by a die roll. You are presented with choices that are essentially meaningless. The only choice you make is how much "explore" successes you can generate based on who you sent on the mission. Often these are trivially easy because of the range of options, in our game no one ever failed a single mission. Then when you succeed? No resolution text, no sense of any real progress or achievement at all, you simply get rewards determined by the book, in a manner that often makes no fucking sense whatsoever. You rolled three successes? -1 reputation and one pot for you! Why does this make thematic sense? *shrug*
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Above & Below offers a variety of game mechanics while keeping complexity relatively low. The artwork is very pleasant, the gameplay is fast-paced even with 4 players, and the bits of stories are interesting (especially when read dramatically). 
My only small critique is that the choices the game presents can be forced at times. For example, when exploring a cave, you are given a few choices to react to the event that was read to you. However, most of the time I find myself choosing the option that seemed most plausible with my current resources, not choosing based on the story that was read.
Overall, it is a great game with lots of variety to keep things interesting.

RATING: 9
Absolutely Charming. Above and Below has a little bit of everything. You build up your village, make tough decisions of how to allocate resources and villages and go on adventures. The adventures play out as a 'lite' version of Tales of Arabian Nights with more control over the outcome thanks to choosing which villagers go. Additionally, adventures feed back nicely into the village building, as even an event that didn't go your way will result in explored caves that you can build in. 

My only negative is that the games have consistently felt too short. A couple more rounds would allow your engine-building to pay off, (though how many rounds more would likely be different depending on how many players you have)

All in all, its a very satisfying game that scratches a number of itches, gorgeous art and is a pleasant stepping stone between gateway games and hardcore ones.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The paragraph-based adventures of Tales of the Arabian Nights meet a simple engine building game.  Ryan Laukat has created a charming world for his games and peopled it with both humans and anthropomorphic creatures.  It's something of a shame then that the adventures in the base game tend to be perfunctory and unsophisticated - meet creature(s) and help/harm/run away.   Success is defined in terms of resources gained, rather than a resolution to the story.  This tends to put the focus on the less interesting business of getting stuff and converting it into victory points.  

Above and Below isn't a worker placement game in the usual sense in that we aren't competing for spots on  a board.  Rather, workers are allocated to tasks and there are some that only specialist workers can do. I have to confess that I found some satisfaction in sending my HR recruitment specialist off to the fields to pick me yet another pear.  I like the mechanism whereby spent workers need a bed to rest in or some cider to drink before they're ready for another day's work.  

Ultimately, Above and Beyond may disappoint both the cube pushers and those who want games that tell stories.  You might call it an interesting failure but that still places it ahead of the landfill dungeon crawlers and the spreadsheet euros.

Production quality is very good and the artwork in Laukat's trademark style is fantastic.

RATING: 7
Άλλο ένα πολύ όμορφο εμφανισιακά worker placement παιχνίδι όπου οι παίκτες φτιάχνουν το χωριό τους σε ένα άγνωστο μέρος και ταυτόχρονα εξερευνούν τα σπήλαια από κάτω. Οι κανόνες είναι αρκετά απλοί ώστε να μπορείς να το δείξεις στον καθένα και το παιχνίδι στήνεται γρήγορα. 
Αρχικά έχουμε 3 εργάτες ενώ τα διαθέσιμα actions του παιχνιδιού είναι 5. 

Μπορούμε λοιπόν να χτίσουμε καινούργιο κτίριο, κάτι το οποίο απαιτεί έναν builder εργάτη, χρήματα και ίσως ένα εξερευνημένο σπήλαιο αν μιλάμε για underground κτίριο. Τα κτίρια προσφέρουν διάφορες ικανότητες αλλά και παράγουν διάφορα υλικά. Επίσης τα κρεβάτια είναι πολύ χρήσιμα καθώς αυτά καθορίζουν πόσους εργάτες μπορούμε να ξεκουράσουμε κάθε γύρο. :meeple:

Μπορούμε επίσης να εκπαιδεύσουμε νέους εργατες, το οποίο απαιτεί λίγα χρήματα και έναν teacher εργάτη.Μπορούμε να κάνουμε harvest στέλνοντας εργάτες σε κάποιο κτίριο παραγωγής μας ή μπορούμε να τους στείλουμε να δουλέψουν και να μας φέρουν χρήματα. Τα προϊόντα τα κρατάμε στο κάτω μέρος του ταμπλό μας και αυξάνουν σιγά σιγά το εισόδημα μας ενώ αξίζουν και πόντους στο τέλος του παιχνιδιού. :)

Τελευταίο και πιο ενδιαφέρον action είναι η εξερεύνηση των σπηλαίων. Απαιτεί τουλάχιστον 2 εργάτες και αφού ρίξουμε ένα ζάρι βλέπουμε ποια παράγραφο του βιβλίου πρέπει να διαβάσουμε. Εκεί υπάρχει μια μικρή περιπέτεια και ο παίκτης πρέπει να πάρει μια απόφαση μην γνωρίζοντας το αποτέλεσμα της κάθε επιλογής. Γνωρίζει όμως τον αριθμό δυσκολίας της κάθε επιλογής. Αυτό δείχνει το πόσες επιτυχίες πρέπει να κάνουν οι εργάτες σου, και καθορίζεται με ζάρια. Ο κάθε εργάτης γράφει πάνω τι μπορεί να κάνει πχ με 1+ δίνει 1 επιτυχία αλλά με 4+ δίνει 2 επιτυχίες.

Στο παιχνίδι υπάρχει και ένα reputation track που δίνει πόντους στους πιο διάσημους παίκτες και επηρεάζεται από τις αποφάσεις που πήρες κατά την διάρκεια των εξερευνήσεων σου

Συνολικά δηλαδή έχουμε ένα απλό παιχνίδι ανάπτυξης με θεματικές πινελιές εξερεύνησης. Τα υπέροχα γραφικά βοηθούν στο να μπεις στο κλίμα. Θα ήθελα η εξερεύνηση να ήταν ακόμα πιο αναλυτική και περιγραφική. Βέβαια αυτό θα στοίχιζε ίσως σε χρόνο παιχνιδιού και downtime. Είναι ένα υβρίδιο μεταξύ eurogame και thematic παιχνιδιού όμως κανένα από τα 2 κομμάτια του δεν σε γεμίζει. Το engine building κομμάτι είναι πολύ απλό ενώ και το κομμάτι της εξερεύνησης είναι λιτό και σύντομο. Δεν ξέρω σε τι τύπου παίκτη τελικά απευθύνεται το παιχνίδι. Μπερδεύτηκα. :what:

Πολύ όμορφο παιχνίδι με χαμηλή πολυπλοκότητα και μια δόση τύχης στην εξερεύνηση. Το interaction μεταξύ των παικτών είναι μικρό και περιορίζεται στο ποιος θα προλάβει να ψωνίσει τα πράματα από το κεντρικό market κάθε γύρο. Θα το ξανάπαιζα.

RATING: 9.5
2015 Kickstarter Expanded (Limited) Edition (proud owner)


A beautiful game! Thick components, very well made!
Have played it once, the gameplay flows really well and the storytelling element will bring something new to the table every time.
When the Expansion stories come out, the game will flourish I think...They will be available on late October, Ryan stated, but till then we have a lot of games in our hands!
Great game, can also be expanded to 1-2 rounds more, because like all of his games, it ends when your "machine" starts rolling...

Apart from that, I think that the game generates endless games and can offer a truly unique storytelling element, which is nowhere to be seen in a previous game, because the results of each exploration can affect the game and the final Village Points. 

That being said, reputation (it can be obtained really through exploring) is not so big a deal in the game, unless two players are really close. Better concentrate on getting the right buildings. The latter, of course, is a matter of exploring. If you do not explore, you cannot build below. 

Above...well, this is just another ballpark. So many buildings to choose from, refreshing the building row is a free action and can really help and, of course, players must focus on getting those key buildings. They are the real Key to the game, where the star buildings, expensive as they are, can boost someone in the endgame or/and steal the win!

RATING: 9
Above and Below is an absolutely fantastic game by genius designer/artist Ryan Laukat. The beautiful art and sensible graphic design on all components is as expected from Laukat at this point, with everything working well and looking inviting. The game itself is a worker placement in typical Laukat fashion, filled with plenty of options and trials to overcome, forcing the player to capitalize on their actions by choosing when to use which villager to do what things. The game centers around gathering resources, either through buildings or exploring, and choosing when to apply what resources to increase your income and end of game victory points. But the game shines when you go "Below," exploring the caverns, endangering your villagers, and clearing out caves to build powerful outposts in. With many roads to strategies, cleverly told stories, and calculated risk, Above and Below is just another wonderful game that can be easily placed in anyone's collection.

RATING: 9
We have owned both Agents of SMERSH and Tales of the Arabian Nights. Let's be honest and call a spade a spade. Tales of the Arabian Nights is not a game. It is a choose your own adventure book trying its best to look like a game by adding a board and dice. SMERSH does a bit of a better job by making it a co-op and adding just a touch of strategy & tactics. 
But Above and Below delivers on what most people believed they were getting with those games. Ryan Laukat gives you an actual GAME to go with the storytelling. Worker placement and resource management are key to this game. And there is a very good amount of strategy to it. Gaining resources gets you more money which allows you to build buildings and more workers to get you more points at the end of the game. The exploration action which is where the storytelling comes in is whimsical (written by Ryan and his friends and family) but makes you plan ahead. You need workers to explore successfully. This game only gets a nine because the stories are not as engaging or immersive as they are in Arabian Nights or SMERSH. It more than makes up for that with the game mechanisms but if the stories had a bit more meat to them this game might make it into our top 10.

RATING: 3
There are a lot of wonderful things to say about this game. The production quality is top-notch, the artwork is fantastic, the gameplay is fluid, etc. But most significantly, it mixes story telling with more modern game mechanics. And it does so where both elements seem to take an equal amount of time during the gameplay. Thus, for gamers that don't particularly enjoy story-telling, there is still an element that may hold their interest. 

But, I am not this game's audience. I don't enjoy storytelling games and the gameplay mechanics introduce more randomization/luck than I enjoy. For a family gamer looking for something that an adolescent could play and something that wouldn't bore an adult... there is something unique and refreshing here. But for a gamer like me, who is looking for something more competitive/brain-burning/etc, this isn't the game for you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The “Above” part of this game is a lightish but tight civ engine builder. You're starting a new village and will want to recruit more villagers to perform tasks such as harvesting, which produces goods that increase your income each turn and give end game VPs. But your extra villagers will require you to build more homes with beds so they can rest from their labours.

So far so normal, though it is worth adding that the artwork is lovely, with your house cards satisfyingly abutting each other in a line as your village expands, with the dark caverns below them.

What sets this game apart is the “Below” element. Underneath your village there are caverns you can send your villagers to explore. If you do so, another player will read aloud a short randomly selected tale from the Encounter Book, which will usually give you a couple of options (eg steal the gold from the defenceless old lady or help her find food; fight the giant rats or run away). You choose an option, and a dice roll plus the ability of your exploring party and some useful luck mitigation decides the outcome.

You could ignore the underground exploring if you want and concentrate on building your above ground village but this will have disadvantages. One route to VPs is advancing along a Reputation Track, which you can only do by exploring, and the encounters if successful can yield money and goods, and allow you to build underground outposts that are cheaper than equivalent homes in the village.

One other element worth mentioning is the way you place goods on a tracker that produces a neat income/VP dilemma: do you want an immediate increase in income, or delay that benefit in the hope of more end game VPs?

Some gamers fond of soulless cube-pushing exercises might decide that the story-telling annoyingly slows down the game (wife's verdict on the stories: "superfluous"), others may think that it transforms a gentle but not exceptional civ builder into something unusually atmospheric, it's just a question of what suits your tastes.

RATING: 5
Updated review, original remains below.
In my first 3-5 plays I thought there was "some" imbalance. After ten more plays I've realized the game is completely imbalanced, fundamentally flawed, and feels incomplete. First, in terms of balance, there are 2-3 buildings that basically win you the game for building them, and because they are "Star Buildings" they will appear every game. One is worth 12 victory points on it's own. Building this dramatically increases the odds that you will be the winner. Another gives you 2 per villager you have and can be abused pretty easily.

Continuing on the balance front, the exploration aspect is more or less broken. The stories have uninteresting ends, but even more important, there are decent odds that you will spend 2 of your precious actions to get nothing or even lose something from exploring. In my last game I sent 2 characters to explore, rolled successful rolls, and earned the prize of losing two coins. What a game. Punishing the player for engaging in the most interesting aspect of the game.

Aside from this, income is too limited, actions are too limited, and strangely enough the game feels too short. By the time you finally get a decent engine rolling, the game ends. As a result the game feels unfinished. Either they should have made your starting position slightly stronger, or they should have added a couple of rounds. Unless you use a couple of specific strategies, by the time you've dug yourself out of the game's awful starting spot the game will end.

It all adds up to a big shame. This game has some of the most beautiful art I've seen in gaming, and had the stories been better fleshed out it could have been one of the best narrative experiences in light board games as well. Instead you end up with a game that dabbles in too many areas, does none of them well, and ends too soon.

After 5 plays I never wanted to play it again. I've only played it as much as I have because I put it into two "play X times" challenges expecting it to age better.

Original:
Fun game. It does run short on replayability as the stories get told over and over eventually. There also seems to be some imbalance in the mechanics, but in the end, it's still a fun game.

RATING: 6
It started off strong with our first play. It created memorable stories to talk about afterward but that has faded now. What was once an enjoyable minor city builder with adventure aspects turned into a greedy resource hoarding game. Perhaps the issue is with the almost frustratingly short game rounds. This has been moved to the bottom of the shelf and is only a fond memory of what it once was.

For now, I find that it is just exactly what I wanted out a lighter Red Raven game, and if that game turns out to be a limited experience, then so be it. It feels worth it for the stories alone, which makes the great game play gravy, so even if I get less than ten games out of this, it will be well worth the cost of admission. If you are on the fence, jump in, what a fun adventure that really FEELS like an "Adventure" even though you are really playing a light euro with very little interaction. What a great disguise!

I'd like to start out this review by saying, that I've only gotten to play this game once so far and only with four players. When playing with four players you modify the first round into something that's extremely short. It seems like a four player game may not be the best number for this one...

On to the gameplay... I had pretty mixed feelings about this. I thought the exploration mechanic and the encounter book was a really neat idea and well implemented. I didn't really like that your exploration total was determined by a dice roll. Some parts of the game work really well together but some parts feel really tacked on. Overall the gamed seemed really short and we had a hard time getting any kind of engine going. However, when I get this to the table again I'll be playing the game completely different. Money is much more important then it originally seemed.

RATING: 6.5
I'll have to give this one more try before I pass my final judgment, but the mixture of traditional Euro mechanics with random storybook elements (which have wildly unpredictable results) is a big miss for me. 

It feels like it's far more lucrative to simply go for self-evident points (i.e. managing resources) than to go exploring, which can often be a waste of time unless you absolutely need another slot for a building. Maybe if players fall behind, the random storybook rewards would allow them to catch up if they get lucky, but I just don't see the appeal of mixing these two genres, one of which favors more serious strategic play, the other which encourages casual play for laughs.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A nice little engine building game, where you build up your cast of characters with slightly different abilities and your array of buildings that grant you various things in order to best collect resources and other things that are worth the points you need to win.

However, unlike other games of this kind, this one builds in a little story telling element, where you can go adventuring in hopes of finding various resources and treasure. Some people may not like this part of the game, as some adventures are just plain better than others, and the results of your adventure will sometimes come down to a die roll going your way or not. I personally don't mind the random element, but some may find it bothersome.

What keeps this from rating higher for me is that the story telling part felt a little weak. The book presents you with various scenarios, and generally gives you option A or option B, which are usually just an easy thing requiring few successes, or a harder thing requiring more successes. The choice is pretty much always an easy one based on how many successes you think you can roll, as opposed to other story-based games like Tales of the Arabian Nights where you're factoring much more into your decisions. I was left wishing the characteristics of the characters, or what items you had on hand played into these decisions much more.

I was also a bit disappointed in the story resolutions. You get a nicely written description of your encounter to begin with, but then after you roll, you're usually just told you gain something like 5 coins and a rope, without any story or narrative about the result. Just a plain, you rolled 4 successes, so you receive X, which feels like it cheats the story part of the game. In the end, you just have to accept the story part of the game is simply a means to randomly get dealt rewards or setbacks in a way that feels somewhat thematic rather than just plain random. Don't expect a nicely fleshed out story that is tightly tied to your situation and comes to a nice resolution.

I'm a fan of engine building games, so I had fun figuring out what combinations of things would best set me up for success. The game stands pretty well on those merits. I could take or leave the story telling part, as that part is less interwoven into the rest of the game than I'd liked to have seen, but it does add a little extra thematic touch to the game.

RATING: 5
An engine-building point salad game with a dicey story-telling subgame grafted on. It is a pleasant enough game, but I found myself not always making the right move just so I could explore the 'below' section more. And that part is kind of underwhelming. 

That subgame presents you with decisions which are all seemingly 
1) meet a guy - do you help him or attack/steal from him 
or 
2) encounter an item, do you proceed aggressively or cautiously. 

Each option involves a number of dice, so you always choose based on the highest requirement you think you can satisfy, and not based on story at all. It's totally disjointed. 

That said, it is all very familiar and pleasantly diverting to play, but just not a great game.

RATING: 5
A standard worker placement, set collection game.  Money was to tight and makes the game a little frustrating.  I like building my city (adding cards with powers).  Exploring overall seemed like more risk than reward.  It is ok as a worker placement game and I would play it again.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This was described to me as "Agricola meets Arabian Nights", which I'd amend to "Vastly simplified Agricola meets simplified and improved Arabian Nights".

The comparison to Agricola is mostly thematic - you're building a community; there's no feeding, starvation, or animeeples. It's not worker placement per se, but each townsperson can only be used once/turn. Your refresh rate is limited by how many beds your dwellings have, which is a nice touch - "spam more workers" is limited in viability by how many you can refresh.

I like the exploration system better than AN in a variety of ways: all explorations involve challenge, you have moderate control over your success, there's reasonable correlation between skill/effort and reward, there's often a relationship between your story choice and the consequences/rewards, and you always get at least one constant known benefit (building site) for success.

The above-ground community-building portion felt fine, though a bit light for my tastes. The below-ground exploration system was a breath of fresh air - I like it worlds better than AN - but the improvements made me hungry for even more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
To be fair this is not my style of game, but further than that I want very badly to describe my impression of the gameplay.

Points were assigned to various actions in a very balanced manner, but the game has no hook to provide thoughtful decision rich gameplay. It is a book keeping like management sort of simulation where the rewards seem to always feel to me like of the "oh now I get a point and one fish" type. These rewards are obviously just tacked on to provide scoring. So that we can all pretend that this choose your adventure game plus a management simulation is actually more of a game than it is. That is my accusation. It is not an accusation that the game is not balanced. My accusation is that Above and Below does not deliver real, fun gameplay; and I am particularly sure about this fact for me personally. I would never ask to play this. Literally most average in skill game designers could design the mechanics of this game, and that cannot be said for a fun math puzzle like Feld or Bauza or Stockhausen could make.

Is it really a game? It should be known that I do not respect this game even for what it is trying to be. It feels like it came from Kickstarter and most all of them that come from Kickstarter do.

Further than all of this the random story snippets offend me not because they are written poorly but because no one cared enough to make them linked enough together. It wouldn't be that hard to make them more linked. Longer chains are needed where one decisions affects the next decision in the game more, or maybe I just don't like roleplaying. I don't like bad roleplaying and this is very bad roleplaying to me.

In the past I played action rpg's and mmo's and currently enjoy Dungeon Quest on Android. At least it has gameplay albeit repetitive. Is there a story? No. But I would take decent gameplay over some sort of bad disconnected group of stories any day. 
If you want to make a story in a game don't play at it. That offends me. And don't undertake ambitious projects with ambitious goals and then end up with ordinary results that seem somewhat made for kids. That is just my opinion and it is not going to be an acceptable one.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Components: Gorgeous artwork to differentiate all the cards and tiles. Fun narratives with quite a bit of variety, even given the subterranean setting. My only two complaints with the narratives are a) most of the characters we meet are male, and b) too many of the narratives have “continue exploring” as a counter-choice to the interesting option. I'd prefer if the villagers had names printed on them, to give them more personality.

Experience: Fast playing. One of the few strategy games that plays in the advertised time on the first play. As the game comes to a close we're actually a little sad, because we're just getting rolling.

A simplified worker-placement game, in that there are only five places to put my workers. I like that I can use actions in any order, and those actions aren't being blocked by the actions of other players. Like other worker-placement games, it's a tight economic game, whereby I must execute my actions in an efficient order.

The encounters are fun, with random prizes. I'm not yet certain that they are critical or even central to the game, which is odd given how much work went into them. But they are definitely the highlight. Mechanically, they almost always boil down to “how many lanterns do I think I can get?”, since there is seldom enough information to glean what the outcome will be from a choice (other than gaining/losing reputation). More is almost always better. I wish the outposts were on the back side of the cavern cards, so that when I explore a cavern successfully I immediately get that reward; as is, I explore for the fun and immediate reward, but I seldom build an outpost because they aren't better than above-ground settlements, especially toward the end when I'm trying to build the start buildings.

Tried a 14-round game; I see now why the game lasts only 7 rounds.

First Played: 2017-10-01
Last Played: 2017-11-24
Plays (14):
2 players: 13
3 players: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Was hoping for a "storytelling" game a la Tales of the Arabian Nights or Dead of Winter, but that's not what this is. It's a very beautiful, simple worker placement game, where the storytelling enhances the sense of theme. I had a lot of fun with it during my play at Gen Con.

My disappointments: the main one is that the storytelling mostly follows the same structure. In each one, you (1) are read a scenario of what happens when you dig and explore underground, (2) are given a choice of what to do in that scenario, and (3) are given a reward for beating your challenge. I would have much preferred you to be read a storytelling result based on your success or fail in each mission, even if it was a sentence or two. "Do you want to run from the spider or fight?" "I want to fight." "Ok, roll the dice....you succeed, here is a gold." If I was read a result after that that related to the story, I would feel like it was a complete game. I also think that the low number of results in the book (I believe less than 200) will lower its replay value.

In the end, I recommend this as a gateway game for new gamers that are interested in fantasy theme and story before trying something like Caverna. It can be a lot of fun with a good group and has really beautiful art. Also, if you want a game like Tales of the Arabian Nights that is more of a real "game" with good mechanics, this should also fit the bill.

RATING: N/A
$20 BGG
Above and Below is a mashup of town-building and storytelling where you and up to three friends compete to build the best village above and below ground. In the game, you send your villagers to perform jobs like exploring the cave, harvesting resources, and constructing houses. Each villager has unique skills and abilities, and you must decide how to best use them. You have your own personal village board, and you slide the villagers on this board to various areas to indicate that they've been given jobs to do.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A pleasant blending of many familiar recent mechanisms, 

At it's core there's a brief engine-builder that is limited to 7 turns; use scarce cash to buy from the tableau. The main infusion to this core is a paragraph-based choose-your-own-adventure, a la Tales of the Arabian Nights which is used every time you explore underground. 

Tacked onto these two main foundations are a prestige track, where your decisions in the adventuring earn you reputation or infamy. There's a Knizia-like set-collection, wherein you gain victory points for variety as well as plenty, And lastly there's a half-hearted trading element, with little structure or attention paid to it. 

Overall I find this a fun, less math-y, and more evocative alternative to games like Agricola or Le Havre, It adds some color and life to a genre defined by dull exercises in calculation.

RATING: 7.5
This is an extremely pleasant game, and I particularly enjoy ee of the mechanisms.

1) The exploration mechanism is the star of the show, and I think there is some nice balance between risk and reward.  I don't think it is at all a "story telling game" as it has been dubbed, but the little stories are more involved than your typical flavor text.

2) The resting mechanism, with three states of worker readiness is very cool and adds an unusual but very welcome element to deciding how to utilize your workers, as well as intersting decisons around building up your "bed" engine. 

3) I enjoy the core concept that you are trying to collect different types of goods, and the most of the goods you add later to your collection. This adds a great element of feeling rewarded when you find a new type of good.  

Overall though, the game feels light for my tastes, and I worry whether it would keep my interest over time. As of now, I'd be very happy to play it some more.  The KS exclusive expansion adds a little more crunch, but also randomness.  KS exclusive gameplay elements are the worst though.  Stop doing this, publishers!

I've played a few Ryan Laukat games now, going all the way back to the original Empires of the Void. I always love the art and graphic design, as well as the worlds he creates.  His is clearly highly talented.  The gameplay is also consistently solid, but so far the total experience has always just barely fallen short for me.  I would very much look forward to the result if Ryan were to someday work with a codesigner or a publisher that could contribute some development expertise.

RATING: 7
Quick comments after one play.

Positive Points
---------------
-Adventuring is fun. The stories are enjoyable and there's excitement/anticipation when going below and resolving lanterns.
- Replayability - the fun in the game is with adventuring and there's 150 stories, you're likely to read 15 or so a game, so there's plenty of stories in there for repeat plays (especially given that our group usually only plays the same a couple of times a year.)

Neutral Points
--------------
- Stories are fun, but there's no closure. You read a cool paragraph, make a decision, then it's like, you get a fish... but what happened... would be nice if there was some closure text for all choices
- Complexity - mechanics are really straight forward, light to light medium game
- Bonus - not sure if this is a negative point yet, but some of the bonus cards looks to be much better than the others and almost essential to winning... needs more plays

Negative Points
---------------
- Not sure on the reputation track... seems entirely random. I fell behind early and had no control over catching up... only thing you can do is adventure and cross your fingers. Wish there was some other way to increase rep... for example, when you work

Overall
-------
Above and Below is a light game that blends together Euro mechanics and story telling fun. The adventuring system is very engaging and lifts the game to one that I would play most anytime. Rating it a 7.25 for now.

RATING: 8
Above and Below is a mashup of town-building and storytelling where you and up to three friends compete to build the best village above and below ground. In the game, you send your villagers to perform jobs like exploring the cave, harvesting resources, and constructing houses. Each villager has unique skills and abilities, and you must decide how to best use them. You have your own personal village board, and you slide the villagers on this board to various areas to indicate that they've been given jobs to do. Will you send Hanna along on the expedition to the cave? Or should she instead spend her time teaching important skills to one of the young villagers. I love games with multiple ways to victory. This game definitely stood out in 2015!

RATING: 6
Everyone seems to agree that its basically what Arabian Nights would be if it were a game. The actual game is still somewhat lackluster. 

The components and art are all very nice. Everyone liked the decision point on goods stacking. The stories book is very nice and the adventures are written well and of a reasonable length.

Unfortunately the stories don't add very much to the game play. Typically you just choose whichever one you can reasonable do. It all seems to boil down more to the luck of the draw than anything else. 

The rest of the game play is a pretty basic worker placement game. You recruit more workers, buy buildings and collect goods for victory points. I will say, I like the beds concept to slow to effectiveness of building more workers. Everyone also liked the decision point between placing goods to make more money and saving the common goods until you can place them on a higher point value space.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
+
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Well, this one certainly gets points for uniqueness. One minute you’re planning to buy a new building or acquire a new worker and the next you’re reading a paragraph to your neighbor a la Tales of the Arabian Nights. I found the game refreshing simply for being different from the usual euro fare. 

The main attraction from the euro aspect is managing your workforce. Only some workers can perform certain tasks, and while you can purchase new ones each turn, they have to rest. That is, you have to purchase new buildings with beds such that they refresh for use the next turn. Buildings also offer resources or bonus powers, so you build your own worker and building engine while still managing to go exploring.

Exploring offers you choices of what action to take without knowing the rewards via a paragraph your neighbor reads to you. Then you roll some dice to see if you’re successful. Buildings that offer reroll powers are a good idea here because you simply cannot afford to fail even one exploration. The texts that you read to each other were entertaining in my first game, however, they’re totally athematic. I mean, you’re told how many successes you need for each choice and make your decision based on what you think you can reasonably achieve. Can’t even possibly get 7 successes? Well then, I guess you’re doing the choice that requires 3 successes.  

So is it successful? The euro elements seem to work well enough but the exploring introduces a big helping of randomness both with what type of encounter your draw and whether or not you’re successful. You can send 3 of your 4 dudes exploring so as to increase your odds of success but only pull a minor encounter that doesn’t pay out much even for succeeding at it. So much for that turn. Since the game is so short – the full 7 rounds feel like the mid-way point – I feel like the person who has the most successful explorations will win. Careful planning and management of your workers can only take you so far. 

It’s a hard game to assess after my one play so far, but I do want to try it again to see how much player agency there is versus luck. My gut says there's 2-3 plays here max before the gimmick wears off.

RATING: 5
The game itself seemed pretty solid - a good mix of different buildings available, the sleep-injured dynamics of your workers, variable worker powers... lots of good game in there.

Then the adventure book.

Dedicate some number of your workers to go try to take on an adventure, without having ANY CLUE AT ALL what the potential rewards might be.  Once you go there, chuck some dice to see if you get anything.  Even in Eldritch Horror you at least know that you're likely to get spells if you do a city encounter in Arkham.  Here, you have no clue at all until after you've dedicated your workers.  It really rubbed me the wrong way, and the story bits weren't even really engaging - just some generic fantasy trope text and then a generally obvious choice between an easier thing with likely crappier rewards, or a harder thing with a better reward.

This is another that I'd play again, but the cave adventures part just didn't feel like they meshed well with the rest of the game.  I usually enjoy Ameritrash / Euro hybrids quite a lot, but in this case I'm not so sure.  I did have fun with it, but it felt like it was missing something to make it special.

RATING: 8
Really interesting mix of worker placement and story telling. I wasn't sure these mechanics would mesh but they do really well. 
I would like to explore this more.

Actually I really really enjoy this one. Its so simple mechanically that it is very easy to just pick up and play. The worker mechanic whereby they become exhausted after performing an action and you must provide them with a bed to rest in in order for them to become useful again is really quite novel and I do like it. 

The 'below' part feels a little like a win more mechanic, as you tend to get a bit of everything if you explore repeatedly however if you keep finding nothing really with your explorations it can serious dent your scoring chances. 

The storytelling part (exploration) is well done but it doesn't take over, each paragraph is a story in and of itself.

RATING: 7
Looks amazing - I love the artwork.  It's worker placement with resources and there is a little deck building in there too (character and buildings) so a good mix of mechanisms.

TO me, the game doesn't feel completely rounded.  By this I mean some of the elements feel underdeveloped.  The deck building element seems a little weak as for all the amazing imagery, there's little of use bar a few symbols on the cards and these aren't character or building specific and can be easily found on many other cards.

More an observation than a criticism, but I found myself playing the story adventures to maximise the rewards more than making the politically correct choice. Some of the better rewards came from making the less thematic choice.

And finally, I think a score pad should have been included and I'd recommend that for a 2nd edition run.

RATING: 6
the only reason this doesnt get a 10 is its slightly simplistic, the randomness of encounters can easily skew it in someone elses favor, and because of the lack of resources, you usually have to plan on what big house to buy right at the beginning as well as your strategy. There isnt alot of room to adjust. 

with that out of the way, play this game! if you are a non solitare kind of person this game might not be for you. Or maybe it is for you! My sister hates solitare like games because she plays games so she can interact with other people. In this game it is possible to block other peoples moves with your own actions, but what really makes it fun is if you have an imagination!

When you go exploring they give you a little story text about your encounter. But the text normally doesnt give a resolution, just a reward. So we like to make up our own stories in relation to the text given and the reward recieved. For instance, my sister command her forces to fight to the bitter end, for the greed was strong, and the treasure chest tempting. But the skeletons out numbered them. They faught valiantly to sedate their leaders greed, but at the cost of their blood... (she injured them to insure succes xo). 

so yeah, its fun

update: this is a fun game, but there isn't alot to it. The game feels a little to light for the time I invest in it. I'll never turn a game down, but I won't go out of my way for it

RATING: 6
First and Foremost, the game is beautiful to look at and the mechanics are sound. Ryan Laukat is truly a prodigy in that he publishes, designs, and makes the art for all his games. The man is truly talented.

What makes this game different than a typical worker style building game, is the inclusion of a story book which adds a bit of theme in an otherwise typical Euro game. The stories are well written and all, but I feel they could've gone more in-depth with what gets presented to you. It usually pretty obvious which path to take and the rewards you get are ok with only a handful actually nabbing you a special worker or the like. 

I'm on the fence on whether I should keep this one or not... A new adventure book, with greater rewards and harsher penalties for exploring the unknown would be great, but as it stands, the main "pull" this game has, is ok at best.

Update: Sold this one off, which hurt a bit cause I like the game portion of it, but the story-telling is just lacking. The game is 90% game driven, and about 10% story driven, which is not what I was looking for.  I hope a really good balance can be found in a game, that brings these two mechanics together in a great way without being too long, and have a strong story-telling element as well.

RATING: 5.75
An entertaining way to pass time, but not much of a game in terms of actually making a strategy.

The storytelling element of the game is when you decide to send some of your workers to explore an underground cavern. It seems like you will want to do this at least 1-2 times because you need an explored cavern card to be able to build an underground building ("outpost"). 

Which fine, that is whatever. Except the swing on what reward you can get from a successful mission can vary so much. At least the options make enough sense--helping someone tends to help your reputation, running away hurts it, etc. But the rewards are random. One mission you might get a low scoring resource you already have, which essentially was a dud turn for several workers, while another mission might reward the player a handful of gold.

This game is just incredibly charming. RATING: 6
Was very excited to play this, but was disappointed in the story part. After much thought I think the reason is that the decision given to the player comes at the end of the story, and then there is no aftermath described, which is a let down nearly every time.

For example, there was a great story about the team coming across a giant cat and having to decide whether to run or fight.  My opponent decided to fight the cat which was a difficult choice as it was the highest lantern count, rolled well and succeeded.  The effect was gaining a rope and a lose a reputation...  There was no explanation, no story about the battle or  where they found the rope, or later found out perhaps it was the pet of the underground people.  

It felt that each story was only half told each time.

RATING: 6
Half-way decent mix of worker-placement and storytelling. The stories are less CYOA, and more RYOA (which is to say, dice-rolls determine which choice you can make, because there's always a better and worse option, and you always want the better one if you can afford it), so they're pretty paltry from the exploration perspective. And it seems like a player who's mainly down to hear a story is going to get annoyed by all the Build-Your-Own-Town stuff going on concurrently.

Phenomenal production values all round, and lots of replayability. It might be your thing, but it isn't ours.

RATING: 9
Very fun game. The encounters (exploring) really add a nice touch to the game. You have a several options each turn so there is some thinking / strategy on how you will allocate your villagers. There is also a little risk in each encounter so you can press your luck and hope for something really good or play it safe and settle for something smaller. It is great if your group really gets into the encounters and makes them a part of your village identity. I now regret not backing Near and Far. Above and Below is a lot of fun and plays in less than an hour for 2 players.

RATING: 8
My initial rating:9

January 16th 2017 (changed rating from 9 to 10): 

I will never change my rating! This game is now my favorite to play. It is easy to play, no analysis paralysis, engaging rich stories to read, economical for euro players, and the art is inviting! I never felt like it was ever boring (Gameplay or story). I am a huge fan now. And best part is that my fiancée loves it. She was never enticed by the other games in my collection, but this finally has her excited!

This seriously is a work of art; in game design and aesthetics.

January 10 2018 I think it's still a great game that can be kept with Near and Far. This gives me that village building satisfaction. But I don't think it's my favorite game of all time. But I can still see myself still playing this in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A unique mix of story look-ups and resource engine building. The two parts of the game (exploration and building) do not feel particularly integrated. The exploration is enjoyable, although the writing of the story paragraphs is uneven. A couple of things for this game that struck me:

One, the exploration is the most fun part of the game, but unless it is a specific goal for you, it won't win you the game. In fact, the rewards are often minimal and pedantic and I debate whether they are worth the risk. It is easy to get sucked into doing what is fun, but it doesn't really give you points. If you are lucky you'll get an indirect bonus, but the main thing is getting a building site out of it. This seems strange to me.

Two, I didn't feel like any of my decisions were all that interesting. There were no interesting quandaries to figure out or greatly differing strategies or no affects by other players that forced me to rethink things. Early in the game you target a bonus card to acquire and you start building and collecting to support the bonus you plan on taking. This is pretty much your strategy because there isn't room to switch gears and diversifying (in resources or workers) doesn't reap much in return. So you pick pretty early what your specialty for that game will be and spend the game maxing out on it. Exploration is a push-your-luck mechanic that you should try to minimize your exposure to as much as possible (unless exploring is your goal). This makes the game feel pretty static and scripted once your path is chosen. With only 7 rounds, you'd better choose your goal quick!

Every turn I didn't go exploring I felt disappointed. No adventure! Every turn the game was asking me to make a choice: go have fun or win. I've never had a game ask me that before.

The story piece feels under-developed to me personally and the rest of game is quite standard. As with all of Laukat's games, the artwork is outstanding.

RATING: 5
NOTE: You can read and comment on these thoughts on my blog: https://boardgamegeek.com/blogpost/48839/above-and-below-sortof-reveiw 

You stumble into a cavern filled with the reverberating sound of stone dice being tossed. Huddled around a central table made of polished obsidian are four grayish figures you recognize as glogos. Hearing you approach the nearest glogo gives you a fleeting sideways glance and a dismissive nod before returning their attention to the contents of the table. 

As you observe them you realize they are playing a game involving dice, the goal of the game eludes you. 

[b]STAY AND WATCH.[/b] [o](see break 1)[/o]

[b]TAKE ADVANTAGE OF THE DISTRACTED GLOGOS AND ATTEMPT TO STEAL THEIR STUFF.[/b]  Explore 2, Explore 8 [o]Explore 2 (coin, reputation -1), Explore 8 (four coins, rope).[/o]

[b]Failure to steal:[/b][o] As you reach for one of the unattended backpacks you wonder where your life went so wrong. Distracted by this thought you accidentally spill the contents of the backpack across the ground, several boxes with unintelligible words scrolled across them catch your attention: [thing=168609]Artifacts, Inc[/thing], [thing=147253]The Ancient World[/thing], and [thing=98347]Empires of the Void[/thing]. Hearing the clatter of boxes the glogos turn and advance on you. As you're running away you take comfort in the knowledge that you didn't leave anything valuable behind. [/o]


------------- break 1 -----------------
[o]You patiently observe the glogos and after a time determine the structure and goal of the game. The goal is to gain the most points but the glogos who are most engrossed in this aspect of the game appear frustrated. They are often complaining that the random nature of the game means they cannot adequately plan or strategize. Their counterparts however are equally engrossed in the brief moments of storytelling and appear just as unsatisfied. Each time there is a story element they excitedly flip to the appropriate passage and with great theatrical flair read a snippet of story. At first they seemed pleased but as this is repeated you notice that the bland predictability of the text is annoying them. They become bored with the choice to HIDE or FIGHT, CONVINCE or FIGHT, PROVOKE or BE PROVOKED. Additionally they seem disappointed by the lack of a connecting narrative.

[b]DISCUSS WHO YOU THINK WOULD ENJOY THIS GAME WITH THE GLOGOS.[/b] Explore 3, Explore 5 [o]Explore 3 (see break 2), Explore 5 (see break 3)[/o]
[b]
OFFER TO BUY THE GAME FROM THE GLOGOS.[/b] [o](-3 coins, [thing=172818]Above and Below[/thing])[/o]

[b]Failure to discuss:[/b] [o]The glogos become furious with you for criticizing their game. It seems that even though they clearly don't enjoy it much they don't want to hear you point out the reasons why. [/o][/o]

------------- break 2 --------------
[o]You embark on a meandering and ultimately fruitless conversation covering topics such as: how to derive value from a game, different ways to value a game, what is a game, what makes a game good, and for a brief moment (which made sense in the context of the conversation) what is the air-speed velocity of an unladen swallow. Having learned nothing you leave the glogos to "enjoy" their "game".

[b]SMIRK WITH ARROGANCE AS YOU LEAVE.[/b] (-2 reputation)[/o]

-------------- break 3 ----------------
[o]You quickly realize that this conversation is going nowhere and begin to agree with everything the glogos are saying, even the things that contradict the other things. It's a small sacrifice to maintain good relations with these notoriously violent cave dwellers. Perhaps your goodwill will be rewarded the next time you meet, or perhaps it will be as if this encounter never happened.

[b]FEEL GOOD ABOUT READING ALL THE FLAVOUR TEXT DESPITE THE COMPLETE LACK OF IMPACT IT HAS ON THE "GOAL" OF THE "GAME"[/b] ([thing=172818]Above and Below[/thing])

[b]WISH THAT THE STORYTELLING COULD HAVE BEEN DONE BETTER[/b] (An RPG, or perhaps [thing=34119]Tales of the Arabian Nights[/thing])

[b]WISH THAT THE GAME PLAY WAS MORE REWARDING[/b] ([thing=146021]Eldritch Horror[/thing])[/o]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.8
Unique blend of light engine-building, pseudo worker placement, and charming (but basic) story-telling/adventuring. Overall, a good game, but a few issues keep it from being a great game.
1) Roll-to-resolve during Explore actions is like "fingernails on a chalkboard" to me. Even with an option to boost poor rolls, random failures can still happen and are completely demoralizing, especially when a failed Explore in the early rounds all but guarantees you can't win the game.
2) The game ends just as the activity in your village starts picking up. Understandably, the game is designed to be an engine-builder with a limited scope to ensure a reasonable play duration, but it feels like the game winds down a little too soon.
3) Although there are multiple, viable paths to victory up through mid-game, the endgame requires a single strategy to be in contention for first place (in my limited experience, winning requires hoarding money over the last few rounds in order to purchase a star building).
4) After several plays, the stories start to become repetitive and predictable.

But, as it stands, it's a 9 at the very LEAST. Beautiful art. Simple, efficient engine mechanic. This is NOT a worker placement game. So many folks within BGG have been writing that it is. That makes me wonder if they even understand what a WP game even is. The storytelling aspect - while minor - really adds quite a bit of theme to the game. Ryan has turned me into a believer of his work.

RATING: 9
Worker assignment with tight choose-your-own-adventure roleplaying.

To be clear: No expansions are necessary for this to be a blast. However, I look forward to expansions adding to the story and to the variety of strategic paths (and to be clear on that point, too: I believe the strategy has abundant variety, but the basic paths to victory are only two or three and that could be expanded).

Also: I do not think roleplaying skills are in anyway necessary to the fun or execution of the game, however, letting loose with our own attempts at improv was incredibly fun! It's a very safe environment to practice the skill.

The juxtaposition of the strategic resource collection with the choose-your-own-adventuring is not only theoretically awesome but is actually awesome.

Highly suggested.

RATING: N/A
Above and Below is a mashup of town-building and storytelling where you and up to three friends compete to build the best village above and below ground. In the game, you send your villagers to perform jobs like exploring the cave, harvesting resources, and constructing houses. Each villager has unique skills and abilities, and you must decide how to best use them. You have your own personal village board, and you slide the villagers on this board to various areas to indicate that they've been given jobs to do. Will you send Hanna along on the expedition to the cave? Or should she instead spend her time teaching important skills to one of the young villagers?

RATING: 6.5
There are some clever solutions here, both mechanically and artistically. Exhausting characters to bed is both intuitive and lovely and while I'm not personally big on set-collection, here it's at least very functional in how it defines your income. Though it also means one resource is as exciting as every other, which is not very.

The adventure book is nice and all, but it's a very random addition to the game. I feel it lacks some kind of influence over going underground. Say I only want some mushrooms, it shouldn't necessarily be as troublesome as going spelunking for ore.

I did get the kickstarter-exclusive book and chits and they do help with more options and a better game, so it's a shame they're not readily for sale (rating should probably reflect this 0.5-1 step higher...).

It's nice and comfortable, but I can't help feeling it should aspire to be something just a tad grander.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The storybook was a nice idea but I don't think that well integrated. While there does seem to be enough incentive to explore fairly frequently and also there is consistency between the stories' universe, the non reputation and cavern rewards felt really random. Not necessarily in an unbalanced way, but in terms of what goods tokens and money you got. That all the tokens do the same thing really hampers the exploration feel, making me generally never really excited about my reward. Outside the flavor text it felt more like a speculative euro resource gathering method rather than exploration. I also didn't like you couldn't really choose what type of adventure you went on or it didn't build on your previous adventures, preventing the generation of an overarching narrative. Still enjoyed it, but much room for inprovwment.

As a euro game, the way the workers work in terms of their strength in exploring and being able to build and recruit, along with the way they refresh through beds felt polished. The set collection of goods however felt pretty pasted on, and the lack of a sliding buy row for the buildings I felt like made acquring the optimal one too swingy. Overall not bad but kind of mediocre as a medium-light euro.

As always Laukat's art is great, and here the iconography was quite intuitive.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Ryan Lauket has a real penchant for beautiful designs. It isn't just his amazing artwork that makes them beautiful, they tend to be clever and elegant takes on well known systems. Above and below is an action selection style, engine building, euro, with  a choose your own adventure mechanic used for exploration. most of your available actions involve maintaining and utilizing your engine through building cards and recruiting new characters, which give you more actions per turn, however when you go exploring in the caves below your village you are treated to a story choice that, if made correctly, will grant resources, special character, and sometimes more story based on how you reacted. The whole thing comes together much better than it probably sounds, and I find it to be easy to get to the table even when playing with people who normally consider euro's to be a little dry for their taste.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The thing I want to say about Above and Below is that it's more fun than its mechanics. The mechanics are actually very solid, though the star buildings can make play a bit rote - integrating story with a euro in a fair way isn't easy, and they pulled it off, and the decision to place or wait on resources is wonderful.

The story is fine, though all the excerpts don't really fit together in a coherent whole.

But back to my main point, this game is more than the sum of its parts. Why? Joy. Something about how you grow your village, explore and have wacky adventures just brings joy to the table. It's the euro where people are actually smiling while they play.

RATING: 6.8
I have wanted to play this for a long time, and just recently managed to play a 2p game.

Though people say you don't need to explore to win, I felt I could do little else. Everything in the game costs money and money is scarse, especially in the beginning. I would constantly end up with 2 or more workers that could do nothing else in the round, so I sent them exploring. This did give me great advantage though, since, though I lacked the money to build anything on them, exploring gave me lots of space to build things and lots of goods, that I would immediately place on my score track to make a little more money next time.

I felt, however, that the game was playing me rather than me playing it, as the ramdomness of the encounters made any tactic kind of futile.

Overall the game is good, beautiful components and many strategies possible to try to amass some points. I might have built up my expectations since I wanted to play it for so long, and the game did not meet them.

I am still thinking of buying it though, just looking to see if maybe Near and Far makes a better attempt at it.

RATING: 7.5
One of Lauket’s best, save for maybe this game’s sequel, Near and Far.
Combines euro elements with some storytelling. That being said, don’t expect a super immersive storytelling experience, as the “choose your own adventure” type mechanic of making a choice and then rolling for it does little more than put some flavor around your resource gathering roll. It’s not akin to an rpg, in my opinion.
That said, the mechanics are solid, especially the incentive to acccumulate as many different types of resources as possible. 
As long as you don’t lean too heavily on the narrative aspect, this is a great game. Looks great too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good:
Exploring underground locations results in adventures that are read from a journal. Your explorers get to chose how to handle what they find and then roll to see the results of their choices. This is our favorite part of this game.

Beautiful and diverse artwork. 

Great component quality. 

Bad:
There needs to be more story entries. We played this with 4 and by the 3rd game we end up with a majority of the exploration entries repeating. Once we knew the possible outcomes the game lost some of its appeal. 

There are some buildings which offer a pretty large advantage creating a runaway leader problem. In the games we played the people who would pick these up first would end up winning the game.  

So initially this was an entertaining title, and I wouldn't mind playing it on occasion, but without the draw of unique story entries this one no longer hits the table.

RATING: 5
I don't care for the artwork in this game and I'm noticing I'm having that issue in a lot of the newer games.  It seems like the artwork has declined over the years in boardgames.  Maybe it's just my personal preference.  

Also, the theme of the game to me is really lacking and the stories are lame.  There's no intrigue.  It kind of reminds of someone trying to interestingly describe a child playing a computer game and falling flat.  

There are also some mechanics that don't mesh with me either; i.e. injure your person to get an extra lantern, your automatically replenishable item doesn't replenish if it isn't harvested, a fixed 7 rounds of play no matter how many players, and only 1 additional starting coin for the 4th person in a 4 player game.  Overall, this game just doesn't do it for me and I have no inclination to play it again.

RATING: 7
Not BOTH a strategy game AND a story-telling game, but HALF of each, if that makes sense.  The strategy seems pretty shallow (which isn't saying that the mechanics are bad- they work quite smoothly).  The stories are a very fun diversion, but aren't connected in any way, and you almost never get resolution from the encounters, so you just have to imagine an ending.

If you don't mind both elements being somewhat half-baked, and play just for the fun of it, you might find an enjoyable time.  I would still say I legitimately had fun. So, a great game for casual gamers, as long as they don't mind that it takes about the same time as a more in-depth strategy game (1-2 hours).

Oh, and gorgeous art, of course. :)

RATING: 6
This isn't a very good design, but it is a fun game. It almost feels like two halves of two different designs. You have a Tales of the Arabian Nights-esque choose-your-own-adventure experience coupled with a pretty basic resource management Euro. The problem is, neither half feels completely finished. It literally feels like they smashed two half-finished designs together, and it shows. The game either needs to go on longer, or needs to be fleshed-out more. There's almost no time to enjoy what you've built before the game ends. I enjoy Above and Below, but it's not going to win any design awards.

RATING: 8
Players are workers in a village constructing buildings and exploring the caves underneath. The novelty in this game, is that when one takes an explore action, you consult a manual, some flavor text is read, and the person taking the action has to make a decision on which choice to take. That person then rolls off for success. 

Other actions are recruit, build, and harvest. Another neat mechanism is that once used, your workers get tired, and they need rest through sleep, potions or cider, so you have to worry about that as well. 

There are a number of different goods in the game, and you score points at the end on the diversity of goods that you have. Your income is also greater with more diversity. 

The cards all help you supplement your strategy.  It's fun to play, especially to explore. But exploring has some limitations, since the reward is unknown until you commit your workers and is hard to plan around. But you need to successfully explore to be able to construct below builidings. I am looking forward to more plays. It's very innovative with the explore action and there are various ways to approach the game.

RATING: 6.5
Maybe my gateway for the worker placement genre. The choices seem quite balanced, like taking labor just to get a single coin, but also getting cider if you are first to do so. The choices when exploring have a nice push and pull too. You never really feel like one choice or the other is the "correct" one and in many cases the rewards for a success feels varied. Plus, even though you may feel out of it, there's ways to wiggle your way back into competition.

Ryan knocks it out of the park with the art. I'm hoping to get the extra pieces from the Kickstarter to amp it up another notch. But the buildings all feel unique. You grow attached to certain villagers. That is near perfect.

My biggest irk is the lack of total choices to place workers. Coming from A Feast for Odin with its 60+ options, Above and Below feels restrained in comparison. It makes for easier choices, but easier isn't always better. Overall, gotta keep playing to see where it stands. Right now, it's really good, but not great.

RATING: 8
What this game does really, really well is balance the line between downright fun and careful strategy. Sure, it won't be for everyone: the strategy elements are somewhat transparent, and the storytelling lacks any real decision. But the critical negotiation element of the game somehow bonds it all together and - perhaps more critically - gives it the right amount of familiarity and interaction to be worth playing again.

You could never take it too seriously, but as the definition of a game, Above & Below ticks all the boxes.

RATING: 8
[b]Above and Below[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I enjoyed the combination of story telling combined with a solid, if relatively simple, Euro game. This blows Tales of the Arabian Nights out of the water for me since there is actually a game to play while also enjoying the story as it unfolds. I came close to ordering it when I first played and then, quite honestly, forgot about it. I think my family would enjoy this and I will probably take a flier on it at some point. I would definitely like to play again, especially with my kids. This would probably make a great family Christmas gift this year.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Beautiful artwork, I really like the different species of workers Ryan created.
- Quick game play, so even though the game is relatively simple it's enjoyable till the end.
- An amazing combination of game mechanics, worker placement and story telling, that works surprisingly well.
- The highlight of the game is going on the adventures, if you play with the right group you can really flesh out the stories as you play.

I had a blast playing this game. When you first start playing it is a pretty standard worker placement game with the biggest difference being that only certain workers can carry out some of the actions [i](hiring workers and building buildings). 

I'm only going to focus on what sets this game apart for the rest of this write up, which is the adventuring. You can decide to send 2 or more workers on an adventure in the caverns under the city. When you choose to go on an adventure you read a passage from a huge book and decide what you are going to do. You will always have some options, most of the time you can choose whether to be helpful/just or selfish/evil. On it's own this is a fun mechanic, but if you play with the right group (I did) it becomes even more fun. We were using different voices for the characters and embellishing some of the stories as we read them. As we played we kept embellishing the stories of our band of adventurers, of course this will completely depend on the group you play with. If the storytelling aspect doesn't appeal to you I think Above and Below will lose a lot of it's charm. For me this will probably be a game I get to play with my family. We love to role play the characters we play in almost any game, so introducing them to a story telling game will be quite fun.[/i]

RATING: 7
規則:
*有星房卡開著放一列，9張鑰匙房卡洗勻抽四張開著放，其他移除，名譽版放中間，特殊工人放一旁，正常工人洗勻開五張放上面，回合標記放起始格(有月亮的洞穴格)，放一個酒桶資源在名譽板左上酒桶區，洞穴卡洗勻放一旁，房子跟要塞卡分別洗勻開四張
*每人拿一個起始版圖，7圓，一張起始房子卡放版圖右上旁邊，3個不同類型的起始工人放圖板左邊(背面有房子)，4人時只有有槌子的放左邊，其他兩個放使用後區(圖板有月亮)，每人拿一個對應自己圖板顏色放名譽軌火把起始格，隨機起始拿起始卡(兩人時尾加多一圓)
*遊戲總共進行7輪，每輪由起始開始輪流行動，直到所有玩家都跳過後該輪結束，每次玩家回合有個5行動可選，每個行動前還可做任意數量的免費行動
1.探索  抽一張洞穴卡放在個人版圖左側，放兩個以上工人到上面，然後丟骰根據骰點對應卡上數字，左手玩家翻探險書對應欄位念出遭遇，包含選項跟探索數字需求，但不唸括弧內獎勵，之後主動玩家選擇一個選項後，為每個工人分別丟一顆骰，骰值超過對應工人上方骰值格式可放置獲得下方的探險值，所有總和工人值大於等於自己選項則獲得獎勵，可選擇把一個工人移動到個人圖板受傷區(十字符號)獲得一個探險值，如果失敗可能會有懲罰，之後把所有使用工人放到使用區(月亮)，成功才把卡片放到圖板右側房卡下方，之後可蓋要塞，失敗則放回洞穴卡底部
2.建造 把一個有槌子的工人放到使用區，之後支付一張房卡右上金額蓋到自己圖版的右方房卡列，要蓋要塞要有洞穴卡，然後蓋在上面，之後房卡跟要塞卡補到四張
3.收穫 把一到多個工人放到使用區，每放一個可拿一張房卡上的資源，資源可放到凸板下方資源列，或是自己圖板區之後使用，或是自己圖板左上區販賣
*物品列要放的時候一樣的要疊起，新的都放到目前最左邊的空格，放了不能再移動，除了增加收入，遊戲最後會根據物品所在位置每個都拿上面左邊對應分數
4.訓練  使用一個有筆符號的工人，然後支付工人卡對應位置的金額拿工人卡到你的使用區
5.勞力  把一到多個工人放到使用區，每個獲得一圓，第一個做此行動的拿名譽版上酒桶
6.免費行動  
>支付至少三元買對手圖板左上交易區資源，一定要用錢買，可講價
>放置物品到自己圖板左上交易區，可移除或交換，上面只會有一個物品
>自己回合可花一元把房子或要塞卡四張放到牌庫底開四張新的一次
7.跳過 之後輪到自己也不再行動
*每輪結束後  輪標記向下移動一格，每格就結束，放一個酒桶到名譽板左上，名譽版上工人往左移填空，再開新的，玩家支付藥水可降受傷區工人移動到使用區，支付酒桶可從使用區移到左邊可用區，一個工人可兩種都使用，之後所有工人可使用一張床來叢受傷去到使用區或是使用區到可用區(一個工人不能使用兩張床)，玩家再根據個人圖板下方物品列最右邊有物品格上方金額拿收入，有些房卡或要塞卡有收入也拿，卡上有箭頭的資源如果有空則補一個，起始標記左傳，開始下輪
*遊戲的特殊工人只有特別的劇情會獲得，名譽軌則根據獲得或失去火把會移動調整
*遊戲結束後  物品列每個物品拿該格上方左邊分數，每個房卡或要塞一分，名譽軌根據名次拿5-3-2分，兩人時只有第一拿3分，如果名譽軌太低會額外扣分，房卡或要塞卡上額外分數加分，平手比錢在比工人數在比建築數
****卡片提示
>獲得工人可馬上放到使用區
>蓋房或探索成功後可獲得一圓
>重骰，上面有幾個符號可重骰幾次
>工人符號上有+，則執行對應行動時，可少花一元
>火把增加名譽一格
>物品符號加小圓圈，則一蓋好就放對應圓圈數量物品在上面
>物品符號有箭頭，一蓋好放一個，之後每輪結束如果空會再補一個
>加符號跟物品在一起代表是每輪結束收入，手符號則是一次性領取
>因為物品數目獲得的分數，在物品列的也算，收入則要在物品列
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
After playing it a few times and backing the spiritual sequel on Kickstarter, I was finally able to secure myself a copy! I have friends who all have different play styles, favorite games and pet peeves. We endlessly argue about whether or not Battlestar Galactica is worth playing and if Euros should ever exceed two hours in playtime, and if table-eating 4x games with miniatures are the Michael Bays of Gaming (all flash and no substance). But we all agree on one thing: Above and Below is a perfect game. It is a seamless intersection of theme and mechanics, the game design is flawless, and has tons of replayability. The problem with most story-heavy games (Ameritrash) is that, regardless of the world or universe, the objective remains the same: kill the monster, get the treasure, save the world, blah, blah, blah. Alternatively, the story means nothing as in the case of Tales of Arabian Nights, where a player's most obvious moral choice is the one that (by chance) complicates your path to victory...it is essentially Chutes and Ladders with telenovelas inserted before and after your turn. With Above and Below, all actions consist of one turn, with the adventure part usually being your last turn for the round. The rest of your time is spent buying buildings or harvesting your resources; all to make your journeys into the world below more profitable and less reliant on chance. The objective is still to gain the most victory points but even the social gamers will like the storytelling part; it's a bit like a campfire story. The turns are swift and everyone is excited to hear what happens to anyone venturing into the caves. Fantastic game and beautiful artwork, can't wait for the expansions and the team's new games.
