RATING: 6
I'm not a fan of the attempted merge of paragraph story with a Euro setting. Story driven theme needs a map and characters. Here you're a Euro-driver, but you can't get on with the game and implement the decisions you've planned - ie the essence of good Euro-ness - as you get held up waiting while other players stop, roll dice, read an out-of-place, out-of-context paragraph with zero relevance to their tableau and the resource/VP engine they're creating, and watch while they then roll (and re-roll) more dice to resolve one of the options it presents. Take that out and the game's mechanics seem decent and attractive enough to warrant replay, but it's more downtime than I'm willing to bear, especially as that seems to be a strong path to victory and thus frequently taken.

RATING: 4
Not a horribe game, but not a very good one, either.  If you remove the story book, the card building and worker placement mechanisms are decent yet very ordinary.  I thought many of the resources, like mushrooms and crystals, to be rather silly since you really did not do anything substantial with them.  The main attraction and hype of the game is the little story blurbs you encounter when you explore.  I found these to be disjointed from each other as well as completely disconnected from the game.  You assign workers to hopefully gather enough lanterns through dice rolls to complete a choice A or a choice B.  Choice B is riskier but often has a better reward.  Much  of the time you will succeed and the outcome is along the lines of "take a coin" or "take a resource" and the the story blurb is just left hanging.  When we played, it seemed the players lost interest in the stories and just wanted to hear how many lanterns were needed for A or B.  I cannot recall a single story from the game.  A definite pass from here on out.

RATING: 8.2
Backed it on kickstarter.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Above and Below is an attempt to combine the paragraph-based story-telling of Tales of the Arabian Nights with a worker-placement resource-collection eurogame. It mostly succeeds at that, though dice-based skill checks will deter the luck-averse. The eurogame portion of the game uses a pleasant open draft system to acquire new workers and places to use them, and avoids the "more workers = better" problem by not having them all activate automatically each turn. The resource mechanism was actually pretty clever, as you want to put different kind of goods in your display quickly to gain income, but also put goods that you end up with a lot of into your display later to be worth more end-game points. I enjoyed this game, and my main complaint is that I wish there was more of it--I felt like I would've gotten more out of the game if I had more time to develop my engine.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 6
I have very mixed feelings about the game. The exploring part of the game is again on the one hand very well done: the randomness in accomplishing a quest is reduced by having the options of picking a harder or easier option and the option to injure your workers. But the rewards are completely random, some are way better than others, but you could also end up getting things you don't even need.
So, I like the mechanics and the idea behind the game, but the results are just not satisfying, robbing the game of potential enjoyment.

RATING: 8
sleeved
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a really tight worker placement game where you don't have time to achieve what you want to do. In the game you are trying to make time to hire new workers, buy buildings, gather different goods and go adventuring. The adventuring mechanism is a big luck element. You send 2+ workers, draw a card, roll and die, and then another player finds the matching result number and reads the story snippet in the storybook along with two reactions the player can react with along with the difficulty rating of choosing that reaction. It's unusual, but possible to completely fail, as each worker sent rolls a d6 to see they contribute successes. Rewards can range from a few bucks, to valuable resources, to special characters. The unevenness in the value of the rewards can give certain players opportunity advantages over others.

The obvious comparison for this game would be Tales of the Arabian Nights, which also uses a choose your own adventure style book, however the two games have very different feelings. In Arabian Nights, you have a character, and when you are presented with a story choice, you think, what would my character do in this situation? In Above and Below, you are mostly looking at the numbers. If one decision requires 4+ successes, and the other has a different result that would succeed with 3+ or 7+, what I'm doing is looking at how many successes I think I can roll based on the workers I sent on the adventure. Not as much at the story choice. This isn't a problem with the game per se, but initially I was thinking that this game would solve the problem of Arabian Nights, where it's mostly story and not much game.

Nobody in my group felt particularly passionate about this game, but I'm excited to see where this style of game goes from here.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 4.5
I genuinely like what Ryan Laukat is trying to do with this game, but it doesn't quite shine.   
I'm interested to see what gets iterated on in Near and Far.

Above & Below is split into two charming, but distinct halves: a euro style engine building section, and a completely random adventure segment that requires you to roll dice to progress.     
The euro-engine building is just OK.   There's a market with stuff you sort of want, and workers you can send to do the things you need.  Nothing new, but it flows well. 
However anything you could spend resources on here pales in comparison with the potential loot you could be getting from the adventure segment... but adventure loot is totally unforeseeable and random, so you can't actually plan around or for it.

But thats not why anyone bought this game.  We're all here for the story telling, which unfortunately falls a bit flat.   
Take the adventure action, roll a die, cross reference book to get a bland story snippet and roll to see if you qualify for the skill check.   There's not even a story resolution!  You could be fighting with a goblin for your life, fail the check, and....... well, nothing.   That's it.

Put these two halves together, and you have an engine builder that's swiftly rendered  irrelevant by potential random encounter successes, and story segments with no payoff or predictable engine building relevance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Early reports that A&B was a euro storytelling game blend were a bit off. There is a lot of wonderful theme to this game. The stories are quirky and fun and give plenty of nods to iconic pop-culture fantasy. That being said, the actions you take outside of exploring are a bit clunky and uninspired. Workers generally do the same thing, and the only thing important about the resources is collecting ones you don't already have, thus, most of the strategy comes from being first to take a specific action each round so you can grab a token or spend less money.

Overall, this does not feel like a euro game. Exploring is nearly essential to a winning strategy, and it boils down to mostly luck. Still, there is plenty to of fun to be had here. Above and Below rests comfortably between Agents of Smersh and Tales of Arabian Nights, and will get played about as often. Just don't take it too seriously.

RATING: 8.5
Heres the bulletpoint review :

- great theme going on here.  I like how it introduces us to a fantasy world that I'd like to know more about.  The glogos, all these folks living underground, the backstory of your villiage being burned.... Seems like there is a really cool mythology here I'd like to see fleshed out.  It lets your imagination run wild and that's fun!

- the stories are fun to read.  See above, lots of "unexplained" happenings that make you wonder what's going on in this world.

- a nice village building/worker placement game under all the story.  I'm still figuring out what types of strategies I want to employ (Other than exploring all the time :P ).

- the art is very different....in a good way!  A very unique style that is unlike other things I've seem previously.  Again, this helps with the "giving the world it's own feel".  Sure it may be fantasy setting, but it's not the typical one, and the art conveys this.

- I want more stories!  I would love to get an expansion that adds more tales to the mix, and one that gives both failure and success texts to really help others "feel" the world.  I try to come up with victory or failure summaries on the fly, but for others this is a tough task (I have years of experience as a DM, my wife doesn't).

- I don't like Kickstarter exclusives :(.  I understand the why I suppose, but I didn't even know about this game until it was too late.  I would love it if the extras were put up for sale sometime.

RATING: 5
This title, along with a handful of others, contributed to my gateway experience with hobby board games. I recall being absolutely enchanted with it, and perhaps I still may find it enjoyable to play today. I simply do not feel as compelled to do so as I once was, as my tastes in gaming have quickly deviated into heavy, strategic board games. There is something to be said about simplicity and innovation, and Above and Below is commendable in that regard.

(+) Quite a novel story-telling element.
(+) The engine building and resource management is fairly straightforward to understand.
(+) Vibrant aesthetics pleasing to the eye.

(-) Seems to end too abruptly.
(-) Storytelling loses its novelty over time.
(-) Lacking in player interaction.

RATING: 10
Whimsical fun when played with literary friends who enjoy choose your adventures, the reputation aspect adds interesting player/character alignment ethical choice decisions. There is plenty of strategic thinking to keep all players involved as the game develops. In response to "too few turns in the game" criticism I have read from opinionated gamers, I think the fewer turns adds to the gravity of risk/benefit decision making; exciting do or die moments. Go big or go home, or languish safely...in last or second to last place. Fun to win and only 90 minutes , one is never really out of the game with a good balance of both deliberate caution and risk taking. This game is a keeper!

RATING: 10
The Encounter Book is a 10 - it's some of the best writing I've seen in a board game (not as good as an RPG, but better than Mice and Mystics, etc.), with interesting options - only wish there were more times when the characters exploring or tiles you have is relevant for helping you succeed.  But I can only give it a 9, because there are some issues with the gameplay - I feel like the rest of game other than exploring wasn't fully fleshed out.  Training is not that interesting - the characters don't have much variation.  Harvesting is way too slow and how you place them on the lower track is not interesting - it's just too hard to get a surplus of tiles to make buying/selling/trading between players to be very relevant. This game really begs to take the Encounter Book and design a better game around, one focused on exploring and trading tiles between players to get the right set collection (more points for more different types, bonus points from buildings or even secret starting goals for having certain combinations of tiles, etc.)  That said, I'm still happy to play this game as is, to get a chance to experience more of the Encounter Book.

Update:  Ok, it's a 10. I didn't even play it, but just taught and GM'd the game, reading all the story elements and it was some of the most fun I've had "playing" a game.  When I taught it, I included a house rule that you could have unlimited items up for sale, rather than being limited to one (minimum cost was still $3 per tile) and you could also trade tiles (like a mushroom for an amethyst) and supplement that trade with $1 or more.  I also encouraged players to send 3 or even 4 villagers on their encounters to aim for possible higher rewards (only 1 encounter completely failed).  These 2 things meant that there was a lot more resources and money flowing and the game was a lot more enjoyable for all.  I have played it myself more times and continue to focus on exploring for pure enjoyment (often sending 3 or even 4, and then bummed when it's not a challenging high reward story), and while I find tile trading easier with my house rule, it is still difficult often due to limited money.

RATING: 7.5
Received in trade from bjorndog.

An interesting game that, much like the similar "Tales of the Arabian Nights," almost tries to trick the players into role-playing by presenting them with scenarios and letting them choose which option to pursue. In this case, it's usually in the form of "Option A, which has a target number of X, or Option B, which has target numbers of Y or Z." The player is informed of what X, Y and Z are so they know their chances of actually succeeding.

Now that I've played it once, there are other games that I've received recently that I'd rather get back to the table than this. But I wouldn't object to playing it again, and I think with the right group it could be a fantastic experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This has become a fast favorite at my household, second only to 'Clank!' in excitement to play. My 12 and 13-year-old sisters love playing this. The gameplay is solid. You're focused on your own town, but there's still a large degree of interaction. Trades are important, and the 3+ image sets a nice baseline (of course, other exchanges and trades take place as needed, but the general rule keeps the general consensus of any one item around 3 gold.) There's up to five market rows at once (Above Houses, Below Houses, Villagers, Key Houses (not replaced), and Star Houses (not replaced)) so there's always something to work for and also worry about someone else grabbing.

The real action that separates Above and Below from other games is the encounter exploration book. It is the game's biggest strength and simultaneously largest weakness. The strength lies in its ability to make each game unique for each person. There's value in assigning more workers to the trip, but using that many "turns" at once for what may be a sub-par encounter is always risky. But it's still basically required, and reading can be as enjoyable as playing, so there's a large degree of group participation that people can really get into. (Even though it's not really interaction, but the resources gained from the journey might go up for trade soon after). But it's also the reason it doesn't get played as much as it could. There's over 200 numbered random encounters, but 50 or so of those are just second parts of other encounter numbers. You'll start seeing repeat descriptions after a few plays. That doesn't necessarily change your action (it's still mostly based on reputation and the probable amount of skill you can get out of the people you sent) but it does dampen the immersion the game strives for. So it doesn't get played as often just to try to keep the encounters from becoming too familiar. It's okay to recognise the encounter in general, but remembering the results does reduce the fun for some. 

More encounters and villagers are only available in completed Kickstarter packages (including another encounter book!!!), so good luck trying to track those down. Non-cosmetic kickstarter exclusives (that are more than just a single piece or card or such) always leave a bad taste in my option.

Still, even without those exclusives it's a solid game. There's many paths to success, and I've seen people win in multiple ways. The star cards offered each time allow flexibility for making the most of the cards and encounters you get. I absolutely recommend getting it, especially if you have a group that can really get into telling a story.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
I love the ideas in this game: two things that stand out are the storytelling aspect as well as set collection element. The storytelling aspect really adds some great theme and provides for some interesting decisions and gives you the feel that you're actually exploring a bunch of caverns around your village. The set collection element is innovative - you add goods to a scoring track, and the main element is that the more different goods you place there, the more income you collect, but if you can wait before placing a certain type of good, they can be worth more points. 

What really reduces the rating for me is that the game felt kinda slow, and I don't think it offers a bunch of decisions for the time it takes to play. You have such limited resources and each move seems to be quite impactful to the ending score that you really have to spend a bunch of time figuring out what to do next. Definitely a title where fewer is better, as it lessens the downtime, and I can see it really shining as a two-player game (I played it with 3).

RATING: 8
This is a pseudo worker placement/story-telling game.  I always use the qualifier "pseudo" for worker placement where action spaces are never blocked off by other players' workers.  To me this is more just action selection with the number of actions you have determined by your worker count.

Each round of this game players will take turns taking actions until all of their available workers have been used or they decide to pass their turn early.  You can hire more workers, build buildings that provide a wealth of additional benefits, harvest resources, collect money, or the big action, explore.  Exploring is where the story-telling element comes in.  When you explore you read a passage from a book and it gives you 2 or more options.  Then you roll dice and based on the workers you chose for that explore action you get a number of successes which determine the rewards (or punishments) you receive for your choice and success rate.

I found myself not necessarily optimally choosing options from the book that best suited the workers I chose but more-so trying to roll play what i would actually choose.  This isn't necessarily a bad thing and actually caused me to get more enjoyment out of the game.  It could certainly end in sub-optimal gameplay though, I ended up winning and I don't think I would have particularly cared if I lost because of this but others might.

The story-telling was the best part of the game in my opinion.  In fact, the worker placement portion was reasonably bland.  There was no real worker placement, that is blocking other players, so strategy and player interaction was kind of left by the wayside unless you happened to hire a worker or build a building before another player targeting it could.  But I enjoyed hearing the stories, role playing my own decisions, and accepting what may come.  This leads to one of my biggest criticisms, the story book seemed a bit light considering that's the selling point of this game.  There are, I believe, 25 story cards with 6 possibilities on each one.  That's 150 stories.  We went between 20-30 of them in one play.  So In just a couple games you could start reading the same story which not only just hurts the experience but could also hurt the gameplay if you remember what options you took last time and used that to color your options the next time around.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
I gave this the same rating as Tales of Arabian Nights.  That is fair and unfair.  This is a better game. Actually there is a game here unlike TOAN but they both fill the same niche.  That snowy winter day when you want to kill some time with family.  With TOAN we just laugh our heads off because we get zany and each play is memorable for what happens to us in it.  With A&B there is an enjoyable worker placement game.  You're not going to have the memorable game plays (character disasters) like with TOAN, but there's real decisions to be made with A&B and it's engaging and enjoyable.  There's room for both in my collection, and it depends what mood you are in on that snowy winter day.  Both my young adult children really liked this game.

* 7 Villagers: (Villager Variety) 3 Lost Villagers, 2 Swamp Villagers, 2 Creature Villagers
* 5 Quest Tokens (They offer a little point boost for being the first to get a certain combo of resources)
* 6 Villager Tool Tokens (Awarded for the first explorer each round. Each round they let you bump up dice by one, train for -1 coin, or harvest twice for a single action)
* 12 Villager Status for Desert Labyrinth / Underforest with PDF. 3 x Poisoned (-1 to explore die roll), 3 x Melancholy (1/3 chance of villager not doing an action), 3 x Greedy (Gain 1 fewer coin in an explore), 3 x Coward (Villager cannot be injured for better explore).
* 18 Event Tokens for Desert Labyrinth / Underforest with PDF. These redirect events to the new encounters in the Kickstarter PDF (60 more stories from the extended version in expanded PDF).
* 7 Supplies Chests: Rewards from Desert Labyrinth / Underforest that allow you to add 1 to explore die. Two are worth 1 VP at end of game.

RATING: 9
Seems like a good gateway game for the worker placement genre. I would call it "worker assignment", as you do assign workers to different tasks, and then they become unavailable for other tasks, just like in a worker placement game. But there are no slots that you can occupy in order to block other players from taking an action.

You can of course be the first to grab a particular worker or building, thus stopping your opponent from getting that particular worker or building. But you can't outright block them from the action.

This makes for a much gentler introduction to the genre than a game where a beginner could potentially not just fail to build an effective engine, but even fail to build anything at all…

It's also a good midpoint between fillers and the really heavy games I already have.

RATING: 5.5
Light resource management game coupled with paragraph-driven adventuring a la Tales of the Arabian Nights. Workers can be put to use building, harvesting goods, recruiting more workers etc., or sent exploring into the caverns below the town. Successfully resolving an encounter paragraph there gives a cave space for underground building and some reward or other (a combination of resources, money and gain/loss of reputation).

I'm not interested in light resource management games but this would be a great one for families. While the random elements are significant, they're no worse than most family-weight games and the paragraphs do a much better job at bringing the theme alive than is typical for games with event cards etc.

Good game, not for me but good game.

RATING: 7.3
tl;dr: enjoyable and approachable

This is sort of a worker placement game. You have a number of workers (with different skills) and actions. You decide which workers to assign to which action. The main action is to go on adventures, but there's also resource and worker recruitment elements that can't be ignored. The adventures have you roll a die to determine a random encounter from a big flip book, sort of like in [thing=34119][/thing]. The biggest difference is that in that game is that the paragraphs depend on your character and current situation, so there is sort of a thread that sort of makes sense. In Above and Below the paragraphs are just random, only based on a card and a roll, so there is no line that moves from one to an other. So in that regard [thing=34119][/thing] does a better job. But this game is a better game as it is way more controllable. There is an actual game. I see [thing=34119][/thing] more as an activity as there is so little control. So don't get these two games confused. They are very different but they share one common idea.
In the end I quite like this game. It's approachable and challenging and doesn't outstay it's welcome.

Initial rating: 7.3/10
BGG scale: 7/10

RATING: 7.5
The game itself is good but not great. My main criticism is simply that the game seems to end the very moment your engine of workers is established. I would probably like the game better if it were to go one more round than it does. Or, I might try starting with all your initial workers awake in the first round even for 3 and 4-player games and see if that helps to get the engine going just a bit earlier so you can at least get a bit of experience in applying it.

What really makes this game is the storytelling. It is just so fun to go on adventures and experience the stories. I do think the stories could be improved by adding a bit more storytelling to the actual resolution of the choices you make --partly as a way of justifying (or just creating a more vivid mental picture of) why you got the rewards or penalty that you did. Honestly, I often times just go adventuring as much as I can even though I now it isn't the best strategy simply because it is the part of the game that is most fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I admire Ryan Laukat a lot.  His games are all beautiful with interesting themes, and he does everything himself.  Yet, none of his games have quite passed the cut with me to stay in my collection, and I'm worried Above and Below missed the cut again even though it's a very unique game.

The game is more or less worker placement where you get money, harvest goods, and buy buildings and workers.  There is a pretty good euro base to this game, and it has a very cool income/scoring mechanic for the goods.  Basically, each time you get a new type of good you can lock it into your board.  By locking in the good, you get more income.  However, once you lock a good, that determines how many points each of those goods is worth.  The more goods you have locked, the more points each new good is worth.  So you want to lock in one of each of the rarer goods early, then collect a bunch of common goods that are worth a lot of points.  It's hard to explain, but very cool.

Then there is the exploring.  You send out explorers and read one of the 200+ adventures.  The stories are pretty good and they usually give you two choices that often fall along the lines of "do you do the evil thing that probably has more rewards, or do the good thing that probably has more reputation."  But they also tell you how hard each adventure is, so even if you are inclined to do one option over the other, you often end up just choosing the hardest option that you can actually accomplish.  Once you've made your choice you roll some dice to see if you succeed.  If so, you get a reward.  If not, you've wasted your action.

I have 3 complaints about the adventures:
1) The choices are limited, and often dictated by the target number instead of the flavor of the choices.
2) The rewards are highly variable and unpredictable, and can drastically affect your game performance (mostly, whether or not you get a good that you don't already have).
3) While the stories are nice, most stories have no resolution paragraph.  They just say you get X coins, or a mushroom, or whatever.  It would be nice if they told you why.

So I'm not sure where this leaves the game.  It's a hybrid with a euro core and these adventures on top.  There is too much euro/strategy for people that want a story game.  Yet the adventures create too much randomness for a euro gamer.  I fear that by trying to combine both design ideas, it's going to end up creating a target audience that is too narrow.

But, my wife likes it so far, and that's the most important test.  We'll see how this looks a year from now.  In the meantime, Above and Below made me try out Tales of Arabian Nights, which I'll talk about below.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Played with the expansion at a board game meetup. The adventure stories, the main driving force of the game, are more quantity than quality, with few descriptive results of what occurs after an action. The adventure stories also tend to ignore your progress, only looking as deep as your Explore count (ex: An old woman wants fish, you have them in your inventory but giving her one is not an option. You must search for them or ignore her). The "mean" options are also not balanced, not only because they give (sometimes permanent) downsides but because those downsides are not offset with better rewards. 

The rest of the game is very standard worker placement without any player interaction beyond moving the market rows (there is also no player interaction in the adventuring portion). The worst part of the game, besides waiting for other players non-interactive turns to end, is that you have to play the worker placement to interact more with the story. The stories, however their quality, are much more exciting than this part due to their fun introductory blurbs and random nature. Even then, adventuring is less beneficial than buying workers/making buildings/gathering. Only one small part of the game directly benefits from adventuring (underground buildings). In addition there are several end game buildings that promote having multiple of the same resource, which is counter to the random rewards from adventuring.

The writing and gameplay are both mediocre. The worker placement leaves you wanting for fun choices and adventuring leaves you wanting for meaningful choices. Neither want is satisfied.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
(TActically high) Above and (strategically way down) Below! This game has so many elements that just can't be planned ahead, which makes your choice of what to do a choice of the odds and not a choice of the mind. Also, let's face it, there is no such thing as an epic, immersive world that's to be told in seven rounds of gameplay. It's not happening. When reading from the book of encounters to me all I remembered was for instance, it's either x succeses or y/y+2 succeses needed. Decide now! It doesn't help that your encounters are just a bunch of random events independent from each other. "Hey guys, I've helped you before. Don't you remember? Ah, I see, you don't". A beautiful artwork alone does not necessarily deliver an immersive experience. What was true for Scythe (at least in my book) also counts here. But Scythe is much more solid as a game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Above and Below is a game by Ryan Laukat, a designer and artist whose art style and games have been impressing me quite a bit lately. The game is half worker placement, half dice game. You start with three workers and three beds. Some actions can be performed by anyone, but other actions require skilled workers. Whenever you use a worker they get exhausted and are retired for the round. In the rest phase you can move an exhausted worker to active duty for every bed you own.

There's a market for workers (but only headhunters can hire people) a market for buildings (but only construction workers can build buildings), exploration (where any 2 or more people can go explore the caverns), harvesting (if you have fields to harvest), and a get-a-buck-if-ya-got-nothing-else-to-do space. Interestingly, if you're short on beds, you might not want to send all your workers out to do stuff, but rather save one of them for the next round.

You have an income every round. Buildings will give you beds, stuff to harvest, points, cool abilities, etc. There are some very expensive buildings that'll give you end of game points as well. There's an interesting thing with collecting harvest goods, where you have to allocate them to storage bins. Once put in a bin, that's the only item that can go in that bin. You get points for every item in those bins and each bin increases your income. But later bins score 2/3/4/etc. points for every good in them. So you really want to put your rare items in the early bins and save common things for the later bins.

All the above ground stuff is fairly straightforward. Then there's the underground stuff. All your workers have some ability to provide lanterns for exploration. Usually that means they get X lanterns if you roll below some number or y lanterns if you roll above that number. But there's also people that aren't very good at exploring, for example, a guy who only gives you 1 lantern if you roll 4 or above. When you explore you drawn a cavern card and roll a six sided die. Each result refers you to a number which you then look up in the manual. There's a little scenario you read which gives you a couple of choices, each of which require a different amount of lanterns. You then choose a path, roll to see how many lanterns each worker produces and claim your reward (or not). The reward is the cavern card (which lets you build one of the underground buildings later on which is usually cooler or cheaper than the above ground buildings) and some random other stuff, money, goods, etc. You can also overexert each worker for an extra lantern, but they'll have to stay in bed twice to return to work.

I liked the game a lot. Money was tighter than I realized. And getting rewards in the caverns was more important than I planned for. One of my exploration turns I rolled snake-eyes and came up short on lanterns which really hurt. The underground scenarios were a little hokey and I found myself wanting to skip them and just get to how many lanterns were needed without the flavor text background stories. But overall it's a solid, very pretty game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun and exciting Story Telling game, which is actually a game as well, and not just a process of telling a story.  Basically you take actions, to gain Money, Harvest, Buy buildings, buy villagers, and explore, etc.  There are different paths to victory, and the game ends up being tighter than expected.  

My only issue, is I feel like the game is too short for the engine building it offers.  I realize the reason to make it a shorter game, but you really have to decide what you are going for.  I almost feel like Harvesting should be one free free action per turn, that would eliminate having a villager have to be used, and would allow to explore more, which is the storytelling element.  Maybe having Villagers go straight to your pool when buying them would allow for more actions.  It just seems like once you get going, you are just preparing for the end, and cant do all that you want.  

All in all I really like the unique games coming from Red Raven games.

RATING: 7
Little to truly love, but plenty enjoyable.  Definitely fits the standard of a 'Kickstarter game' - looks pretty with design not fully fleshed out, though this one is better than most.

The storytelling aspect is lacking.  The writing and lore is fine.  However, results of expeditions are very bland - there is never any follow up story, merely an arbitrary list of a couple of resources gained.  They also felt less... varied, interesting, or rewarding than I'd expect.  This is likely good in terms of making the game less swingy, but has the downside of reducing tension and excitement.

The rest of the game is nothing special, but flows smoothly.  Enjoyable with some tough choices to balance, figuring out what to prioritize before other players get them, etc.  There's probably an extant early-player advantage (at least with 4 players) - the key cards can be quite strong and are limited, so the 3rd-4th player will have far worse choices available.

RATING: 5
I really don't understand the high ratings on this one.  It is only interesting because it is a mish-mash of two different genres.  Each of the pieces is substandard and the mix doesn't appear to be geared toward anyone.

At its heart, it is a very basic engine building game.  Buy cards, get stuff, buy more stuff, etc.  There is nothing interesting in the game's core engine/mechanic.

One of the actions you can perform is essentially the adventure component from Caverna.  But, instead of just abstracting the action, Above and Below forces you to read a paragraph of incredible generic fantasy drivel, and then roll dice to see if you succeed (the results of which are completely random).  These "stories" aren't interesting.  Stuff like "you enter a pit, and there is a creepy lady with a pot of gold - do you want to save her or move on."  I definitely want to move on . . .  

All in all, the game works, but I think gamers will find the "game" dull and "stories" even worse.  It might work well for families, but I think that the "game" is too complicated for young gamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Theme/Art:
It's Ryan Laukat, so the art is beautiful. The theme is great too, everybody I played with loved the exploring mechanic, because the stories you read to each others are great. Some are pretty basic like 'You see a lake with a boat, do you want to catch a fish or keep exploring?', but others are much longer (sometimes they spread over several paragraphs) or give you unique rewards.

Production quality:
Very good, especially the coins are great. Everything is thick cardboard, so it will last some time.

Replayability:
There are key and star houses which are different each game, the other houses, villagers and outposts are different in their order, same for the caves. Also, the encounter book has over 200 stories and a game of 4 players usually contains about 20encounters.
I guess it can get repetitve after 10+ plays, because you will recognise many encounters, but those 10 plays will be a blast!

Depth/Difficulty:
It takes about 15 minutes to teach and it is not very difficult, but it has quite a lot going on and the villager mechanic is very unique. It also takes a lot of space!

Strategy:
You should get some new villagers early on and try to explore a lot and hope you get lucky with your explorations. Or you can simply stack resource houses and fill your advancement track. So you can follow different strategies.

Luck:
There is quite a bit of luck involved, because everything you can purchase (houses, new villagers, outposts) has only a small selection for sale at a time, but the biggest luck factor are the explorations. Sometimes you explore with 3 of your villagers ready to beat everything the caves have to offer, but all you get is explore 5 and a coin and a fish. Then the next player goes into a cave and receives 5 coins, an amethyst and the cheapest outpost available. But I guess that's what you get from exploring dark caves!

Above and Below takes 75-100 minutes to play, depending on the number of players and their familiarity with the game.

I and all the people I played with loved the game and especially the exploration mechanic and its encounters. I backed Near and Far inclouding the new encounter book for Above and Below and can't wait to get it on the table!

RATING: 8.2
This one has really charming art and fun writing. I'm a rather annoying person, so by now I have songs for a lot of the events (Like the hit "Rush Rush with the Glogos") and I have a lot of fun making stupid voices for the various characters. 

I think the only other game that I've played which had me reading through events was Agents of Smersh, but this one wins hands down for me. It's not just the writing, I also like that I get to choose where to allocate my workers, whether to build, train workers, harvest resources or explore caves (my favourite!). It's the one game where I don't even worry that much whether I win or not - though I do try - it's just that much fun to go through.

I did have the stupidest fun when I decided to sing "Ai No Tenshi" from Perfect Blue when some old woman in some event sang her song to her long lost love to remind him of her. 

I was really happy to know that there were additional events in a second book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
What a phenomenal game! All the art is just gorgeous, the encounters are really interesting and varied, and despite only taking 7 rounds (and having only one action first round), it took a surprising amount of time. This game has encouraged me to never hesitate again when it comes to a Ryan Laukat game, because he's a great artist, and the game plays so well.

Our first game, two of us explored like crazy, one person only built above ground, and the fourth tried to invest mostly in goods and buildings that make goods more valuable. The difference in score from first to last was only 7 points, which means there isn't only one way to play to be successful (the top two scores were the two explorers, but only one of us significantly benefited from exploring). We didn't get any of the special characters, so there's still more to see!

I think my only complaint is that it takes up so much of the table, but that's also a positive because there's just so much stuff and it's all lovely.
